<div class=\"widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=25864260 id=\"25864260\" class=\"abstract-title\" >Abstract</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">We report the discovery of a gravitationally lensed hyperluminous infrared galaxy (intrinsic <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub>\u00a0\u2248\u00a010<sup>13</sup>\u2009L<sub>\u2299</sub>) with strong radio emission (intrinsic <em>L</em><sub>1.4\u2009GHz</sub>\u00a0\u2248\u00a010<sup>25</sup>\u00a0W\u00a0Hz<sup>\u22121</sup>) at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.553. The source was identified in the citizen science project <span class=\"small-caps\">Space\u00a0Warps</span>\u00a0through the visual inspection of tens of thousands of <em>iJK</em><sub>s</sub> colour composite images of luminous red galaxies (LRGs), groups and clusters of galaxies and quasars. Appearing as a partial Einstein ring (<em>r</em><sub><em>e</em></sub>\u00a0\u2248\u00a03 arcsec) around an LRG at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.2, the galaxy is extremely bright in the sub-millimetre for a cosmological source, with the thermal dust emission approaching 1\u2009Jy at peak. The redshift of the lensed galaxy is determined through the detection of the CO(3\u21922) molecular emission line with the Large Millimetre Telescope's Redshift Search Receiver and through [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] and H\u03b1 line detections in the near-infrared from Subaru/Infrared Camera and Spectrograph. We have resolved the radio emission with high-resolution (300\u2013400\u2009mas) eMERLIN <em>L</em>-band and Very Large Array <em>C</em>-band imaging. These observations are used in combination with the near-infrared imaging to construct a lens model, which indicates a lensing magnification of \u03bc\u00a0\u2248\u00a010. The source reconstruction appears to support a radio morphology comprised of a compact (&lt;250\u2009pc) core and more extended component, perhaps indicative of an active nucleus and jet or lobe.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;methods: miscellaneous&quot;\">methods: miscellaneous</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: high-redshift&quot;\">galaxies: high-redshift</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=25864262 id=\"25864262\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>1 INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Strong gravitational lenses are striking astrophysical laboratories that allow us to study the structure and distribution of matter in and around massive galaxies and clusters (Koopmans et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Ruff et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>; Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>; Richard et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>), cosmography through quasar time delays (Refsdal <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1964</a>; Tewes, Courbin\u00a0&amp; Meylan <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>; Suyu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), perform highly detailed investigations of early galaxies (e.g. Kneib et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>; Stark et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>; Swinbank et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>) and even detect the first galaxies (Kneib et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>; Zheng et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Zitrin et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>). The number of confirmed gravitational lenses is still relatively low: only some 500 are confirmed to date. With diverse astrophysical and cosmological applications, surveys for new strong gravitationally lensed systems remain a useful endeavour. In this article, we present new results from a novel search for new gravitational lens systems using wide-field near-infrared and optical data. Lens searches in the visible bands are typically optimized for finding blue arcs, representing the images of star-forming galaxies in the early Universe (which are abundant). Searches at longer (near-infrared and beyond) wavelengths have the advantage of selecting dust-obscured or very high redshift sources that could easily be missed in optical searches (e.g. Negrello et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Vieira et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The VISTA-CFHT Stripe 82 survey (VICS82; Geach et\u00a0al., in preparation) is a wide area near-infrared survey of the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS) Stripe 82 with Canada\u2013France\u2013Hawaii Telescope (CFHT)/WIRCam and VISTA/VIRCAM, covering 130\u00a0deg<sup>2</sup> to a 5\u03c3 depth of approximately 22nd AB magnitude in the <em>J</em> and <em>K</em><sub>s</sub> bands. Stripe 82 is also covered by deep (24th magnitude) <em>i</em>-band imaging from the CFHT in the CS82 survey (Moraes et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib21\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib21\" data-open=\"bib21\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Erben et\u00a0al., in preparation) with comparable image quality (sub-arcsecond), as well as the deeper coverage from SDSS itself across the equatorial stripe. The combination of moderately deep optical\u2013near-infrared imaging over a wide area is ideal for searches for strong, \u2018red\u2019 gravitational arcs. After serendipitously finding a handful of promising lens candidates through visual inspection, we decided to pass the data set to <span class=\"small-caps\">Space\u00a0Warps</span>\u00a0(Marshall et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>; More et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). <span class=\"small-caps\">Space\u00a0Warps</span>\u00a0is a citizen science project to discover gravitational lenses run as part of the <em>Zooniverse</em> family<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn1\" data-open=\"fn1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>1</sup></a> of online projects (Marshall et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>; More et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Gravitational lens discovery remains a labour-intensive process, even automated algorithms produce highly impure samples that require visual inspection for verification (Marshall et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>; More et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Gavazzi et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Chan et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>; Brault\u00a0&amp; Gavazzi <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). <span class=\"small-caps\">Space\u00a0Warps</span>\u00a0was conceived to mitigate this issue for gravitational lens searches in the era of large surveys which have the potential to increase current sample sizes by several orders of magnitude. Through an online interface, users (the general public) are presented with tens of thousands of images, each of which are inspected for evidence of strong gravitational lensing. A sophisticated training scheme using images containing realistic simulated lenses, built into the inspection interface, is used to simultaneously train users and calibrate the classification process (Marshall et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>; More et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2015</a>). Humans are highly proficient at detecting the \u2013 often very subtle \u2013 features associated with gravitational lenses, and so harnessing the power of a natural neural network of several tens of thousands of \u2018nodes\u2019 is a particularly efficient approach to this problem.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The VICS82+CS82 search with <span class=\"small-caps\">Space\u00a0Warps</span>\u00a0(actually the second incarnation of the project, the first was an optical search within the CFHT-Legacy Survey) was accelerated by being launched on <em>Stargazing Live!</em>, broadcast on 2014 January 7 to approximately 2 million viewers in the United Kingdom by the British Broadcasting Corporation. The goal of <em>Stargazing Live!</em> is to engage the public with astronomy-related activities ranging from amateur pursuits through to the communication of the latest scientific research, major missions and projects. The wide reach of this programme (which was run over three consecutive nights) makes it an exceptionally successful and efficient public engagement tool for promoting astronomy, and indeed science in general. <span class=\"small-caps\">Space\u00a0Warps</span>\u00a0was phenomenally successful: by the end of the final programme on January 9, over 7.5 million classifications of images had been logged, such that each <em>iJK</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>-band image in the sample was independently viewed approximately 200\u00a0times. Out of several tens of excellent lens candidates identified by the collective efforts of all participants of <span class=\"small-caps\">Space\u00a0Warps</span>, the best candidate we have appears to be an active, dust-obscured source at <em>z</em>\u00a0\u2248\u00a02.5. Dubbed \u20189io9\u2019 (a shortening of its <span class=\"small-caps\">Space\u00a0Warps</span> identifier ASW0009io9<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn2\" data-open=\"fn2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>2</sup></a>), the target appears as an incomplete red Einstein ring of radius approximately 3 arcsec around a luminous red galaxy (LRG) at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.2 (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"25864267\" scrollto-destination=\"25864267\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/452/1/10.1093/mnras/stv1243/2/m_stv1243fig1.png?Expires=1611467387&amp;Signature=bjHBbW0A39rWWCvIr5jFBeivAk5~tlQ86AgKRcPBa3jc4baZsLXV~qxfq1umGK7tb5xs~1CejWJ7sPWFrSBW1mA72lKaZZonndCsXinmBQ0bbQfzbL8caRD~XSzcVmkgfdPcDH-Mh1J7pKInsajWLZD1IMiSsNGbEk7TxP3ZmjdSXDr2ORnFzkvWKR8jt3HzUGjHjx4j-zGcUAYJp9VXZiKsYt1XdANdoW-Fsg95qrhkxNUw5YNxyB82yhpYa74HKylGwgSqx1MnA4V9xZtT4~rtWcJFYL3ECHnyQz-y8PhcCWv3O0bYuskCRfQJ2~pSJ0D1vAIPhpRJjQsNj~rGYg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The colour composite image of 9io9 that was presented to online visitors to Space\u00a0Warps\u00a0. Users were presented with a (random) sequence of 40 arcsec cut-out images where the blue channel represents i-band data from the CHFT Stripe 82 (CS82) survey (Erben et\u00a0al., in preparation) and the green and red channels are J- and Ks-band imaging from the VISTA-CFHT Stripe 82 (VICS82) survey (Geach et\u00a0al., in preparation). Images were centred on luminous red galaxies (LRGs), groups and clusters and quasars. Volunteers were asked to identify potential lensed features, having been trained using simulated images. In this case, a red ring of radius \u22483 arcsec can be seen around the central galaxy (an LRG at z\u00a0=\u00a00.2). This system was independently identified by several users and quickly became a high-confidence lens candidate.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv1243fig1.tiff\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/25864267/stv1243fig1.tiff\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv1243fig1.tiff\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"25864267\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/452/1/10.1093/mnras/stv1243/2/stv1243fig1.png?Expires=1611467387&Signature=udheomsJiFACjvLcipvYMXdoRsDIxcbX2yqu8rht70B1UL-~QkrE9ZfvxIlGxbbLMtusOKv6kzfUchlWkeJnnAHUC6TFLEkFgdlyTzM56K1-dFOxUTBhZzXbH6ekwt8ltQOiPBmXtJg3cr8UolQrhc5n3eGv3TNBi14UEhQSTZqyoEUWU-RQGdckW5x2H1K55s4Dc4tDpOGPELqmJuFExwMFv4Y8hwptp96DFrmkVASABoA7FhqjvlN~O~1vgYsdM9yseAfGiowoBciWSOIO5W9c9flPcJ9kPDPTVaEEWQ8WmPFT7SNg~yjGUOXDsNhYbZfBF3gRb9SXngKChUjGww__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=25864267&ar=1748425&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv1243fig1.tiff\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The colour composite image of 9io9 that was presented to online visitors to <span class=\"small-caps\">Space\u00a0Warps</span>\u00a0. Users were presented with a (random) sequence of 40 arcsec cut-out images where the blue channel represents <em>i</em>-band data from the CHFT Stripe 82 (CS82) survey (Erben et\u00a0al., in preparation) and the green and red channels are <em>J</em>- and <em>K</em><sub>s</sub>-band imaging from the VISTA-CFHT Stripe 82 (VICS82) survey (Geach et\u00a0al., in preparation). Images were centred on luminous red galaxies (LRGs), groups and clusters and quasars. Volunteers were asked to identify potential lensed features, having been trained using simulated images. In this case, a red ring of radius \u22483 arcsec can be seen around the central galaxy (an LRG at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.2). This system was independently identified by several users and quickly became a high-confidence lens candidate.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/452/1/10.1093/mnras/stv1243/2/m_stv1243fig1.png?Expires=1611467387&amp;Signature=bjHBbW0A39rWWCvIr5jFBeivAk5~tlQ86AgKRcPBa3jc4baZsLXV~qxfq1umGK7tb5xs~1CejWJ7sPWFrSBW1mA72lKaZZonndCsXinmBQ0bbQfzbL8caRD~XSzcVmkgfdPcDH-Mh1J7pKInsajWLZD1IMiSsNGbEk7TxP3ZmjdSXDr2ORnFzkvWKR8jt3HzUGjHjx4j-zGcUAYJp9VXZiKsYt1XdANdoW-Fsg95qrhkxNUw5YNxyB82yhpYa74HKylGwgSqx1MnA4V9xZtT4~rtWcJFYL3ECHnyQz-y8PhcCWv3O0bYuskCRfQJ2~pSJ0D1vAIPhpRJjQsNj~rGYg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The colour composite image of 9io9 that was presented to online visitors to Space\u00a0Warps\u00a0. Users were presented with a (random) sequence of 40 arcsec cut-out images where the blue channel represents i-band data from the CHFT Stripe 82 (CS82) survey (Erben et\u00a0al., in preparation) and the green and red channels are J- and Ks-band imaging from the VISTA-CFHT Stripe 82 (VICS82) survey (Geach et\u00a0al., in preparation). Images were centred on luminous red galaxies (LRGs), groups and clusters and quasars. Volunteers were asked to identify potential lensed features, having been trained using simulated images. In this case, a red ring of radius \u22483 arcsec can be seen around the central galaxy (an LRG at z\u00a0=\u00a00.2). This system was independently identified by several users and quickly became a high-confidence lens candidate.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv1243fig1.tiff\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/25864267/stv1243fig1.tiff\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv1243fig1.tiff\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"25864267\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/452/1/10.1093/mnras/stv1243/2/stv1243fig1.png?Expires=1611467387&Signature=udheomsJiFACjvLcipvYMXdoRsDIxcbX2yqu8rht70B1UL-~QkrE9ZfvxIlGxbbLMtusOKv6kzfUchlWkeJnnAHUC6TFLEkFgdlyTzM56K1-dFOxUTBhZzXbH6ekwt8ltQOiPBmXtJg3cr8UolQrhc5n3eGv3TNBi14UEhQSTZqyoEUWU-RQGdckW5x2H1K55s4Dc4tDpOGPELqmJuFExwMFv4Y8hwptp96DFrmkVASABoA7FhqjvlN~O~1vgYsdM9yseAfGiowoBciWSOIO5W9c9flPcJ9kPDPTVaEEWQ8WmPFT7SNg~yjGUOXDsNhYbZfBF3gRb9SXngKChUjGww__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=25864267&ar=1748425&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stv1243fig1.tiff\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">The colour composite image of 9io9 that was presented to online visitors to <span class=\"small-caps\">Space\u00a0Warps</span>\u00a0. Users were presented with a (random) sequence of 40 arcsec cut-out images where the blue channel represents <em>i</em>-band data from the CHFT Stripe 82 (CS82) survey (Erben et\u00a0al., in preparation) and the green and red channels are <em>J</em>- and <em>K</em><sub>s</sub>-band imaging from the VISTA-CFHT Stripe 82 (VICS82) survey (Geach et\u00a0al., in preparation). Images were centred on luminous red galaxies (LRGs), groups and clusters and quasars. Volunteers were asked to identify potential lensed features, having been trained using simulated images. In this case, a red ring of radius \u22483 arcsec can be seen around the central galaxy (an LRG at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.2). This system was independently identified by several users and quickly became a high-confidence lens candidate.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we present follow-up observations and lens modelling of 9io9 that reveal the galaxy to be an intrinsically radio- and sub-millimetre-bright hyperluminous infrared galaxy (<em>L</em><sub>IR</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a010<sup>13</sup>\u2009L<sub>\u2299</sub>) at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.553. In Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2\"></span><a href=\"#sec2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">2</a>, we describe archival and new observations of 9io9, in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3\"></span><a href=\"#sec3\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">3</a> we constrain the integrated spectral energy distribution (SED) of the galaxy and model the lens itself, and in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4\"></span><a href=\"#sec4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">4</a> we conclude with a brief discussion of the likely nature of 9io9. Throughout we assume a fiducial cosmology where \u03a9<sub>m</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.3, <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\Omega _\\Lambda =0.7$|</span> and <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a070\u2009km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup>\u00a0Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=25864269 id=\"25864269\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>2 OBSERVATIONS</h2>\r\n                    <h3 scrollto-destination=25864270 id=\"25864270\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-1>2.1 Radio</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Immediately upon the discovery of the lens candidate during <span class=\"small-caps\">Space\u00a0Warps</span>\u00a0we cross-checked the position of the source (02<sup>h</sup>09<sup>m</sup>41<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|${^{\\rm s}_{.}}$|</span>3, +00\u00b015<sup>\u2032</sup>58<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|${^{\\prime\\prime}_{.}}$|</span>6) in other imaging surveys. The Very Large Array (VLA) Faint Images of the Radio Sky at Twenty-Centimeters (FIRST) 1.4\u2009GHz survey (Becker et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1994</a>) reveals a 4\u00a0mJy radio source at this position, but the angular resolution of FIRST is too coarse to determine whether the radio emission originates from the background galaxy, or the lensing galaxy. Hodge et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>) present higher resolution VLA (A and B configuration, \u03b8\u00a0=\u00a01.8 arcsec) 1.4\u2009GHz imaging of Stripe 82 that partially resolves the radio flux into a ring that follows the <em>K<sub>s</sub></em>-band light (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>). This motivated us to obtain higher resolution radio imaging of 9io9, which we describe here.</p>                        <a id=\"25864272\" scrollto-destination=\"25864272\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig2\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/452/1/10.1093/mnras/stv1243/2/m_stv1243fig2.png?Expires=1611467387&amp;Signature=zmSn6WpTQ76Q9B1jOK74AZqJuATghCSQj~o-Rki~OFTeEc6SWtsahZzpYrzSkfCleSAtcguiFYN6ec59j07gLBjHcdyVHzWfTmdkDYLEp5nlJJFsIZmC0zRwChYBwElh~W0wpwNiYrrjJz62nq5wCWbIXRGO8V8A2TKxaBdyiuOUuFF0pG3yXj9LulSM~1NV-r2bQngBXFaDd~FJ9wlHsWP7vDp84yX7c15n7SQgS8d4NaeqlWDFVxVKHBVsca-xqr4arufK6afy4DQcaxIXsyV9KxZ6MCs4zEuzcEpX-lsz6Oj9Aiz7z3jqG9OZiqrgMzJUXGr6IK8Q-f1fZ5idZA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Images of 9io9 (aligned north up, east left): left to right: (a) Ks-band image. Our lens model (Section 3.2) indicates that B is the counterimage of the bright, compact component at the southern extent of A.</p></div></div>                    <h4 scrollto-destination=25864273 id=\"25864273\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-1-1>2.1.1 eMERLIN L band</h4>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We obtained eMERLIN observations over 10.25\u00a0h between 1415 <span class=\"small-caps\">ut</span> 2014 January 9 to 0030 <span class=\"small-caps\">ut</span> 2014 January 10 (during the television broadcast). The target source was interspersed with the phase reference calibrator (J0208\u22120047) with a cycle time of 10\u2009min with 7\u2009min on the target and 3\u2009min on the phase reference. Thirty seconds of data were flagged at the start of each scan to remove corrupted data logged during telescope drives with a resulting total time on the target source of 6.72\u00a0h. The data were divided into eight intermediate frequencies (IFs) with a total observing bandwidth of 512\u2009MHz at a centre frequency of 1.5185\u2009GHz. Data calibration was performed from calibration scans on the resolved flux density calibrator 3C\u00a0286 (1331+305) and the point source calibrator OQ208 (1407+284). The flux density scale assumed for 3C\u00a0286 incorporated the latest coefficients from Perley\u00a0&amp; Butler (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) and a standard model of spatial resolution of the source as observed by eMERLIN. These were used to derive flux densities for the point source OQ208 which has a rising spectrum across the observed band (IF1: 0.815\u2009Jy, IF8: 1.208\u2009Jy), with a value at the central frequency of 1.0078\u2009Jy. Estimated errors in the flux density scale are at the level of 5\u201310\u2009per\u2009cent. Residual delay corrections for the data throughout the run were derived from all three calibration sources. After performing phase and minor gain corrections derived from the phase calibrator, the target was imaged and several rounds of phase-only self-calibration applied. The data calibration and image processing was performed in <span class=\"small-caps\">aips</span>. The final beam is nearly circular with Full Width at Half Maximum (FWHM) 300\u2009mas, and the map rms is 16\u00a0\u03bcJy\u00a0beam<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p>                    <h4 scrollto-destination=25864275 id=\"25864275\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-1-2>2.1.2 Karl G. Jansky Very Large Array C band</h4>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Data were obtained during 2014 March 1 using the three-bit samplers and an advanced mode of the immensely powerful WIDAR correlator at the heart of the Karl G. Jansky VLA (proposal 13B\u2013458), with eight sub-bands in each of the <em>A</em>1/<em>C</em>1, <em>A</em>2/<em>C</em>2, <em>B</em>1/<em>D</em>1 and <em>B</em>2/<em>D</em>2 basebands. We used the VLA's <em>C</em>-band receivers with the correlator instructed to record a band comprising 1024\u00a0\u00d7\u00a062.5\u00a0kHz full-polarization channels centred on the 22.23508\u2009MHz water line, tracking a barycentric redshift of 2.5529. We also recorded 31 wider bands to sample continuum emission, each comprising 128\u00a0\u00d7\u00a01\u2009MHz dual-polarization channels, covering 4.05\u20137.96\u2009GHz contiguously, with a few small regions of overlap. To mitigate time smearing, since we used the widest (A) configuration of the VLA, the integration time was set to 2\u00a0s, which resulted in a total data rate of 62\u00a0Gb\u00a0h<sup>\u22121</sup>, with 26\u00a0\u00d7\u00a0287\u00a0s (roughly 2\u00a0h) spent integrating on 9io9, 27\u00a0\u00d7\u00a031\u00a0s on the gain calibrator, J0215\u22120222, and 226\u00a0s on 0137+331 (3C\u00a048) to set the flux density scale. The data were calibrated using version 4.1 of <span class=\"small-caps\">casa</span>, starting with a pass through the initial stages of the VLA data pipeline, then flagging extensively by hand, then re-starting the pipeline where we had previously paused. <span class=\"small-caps\">casa</span> was also used for imaging, which required us to map and clean a number of bright, nearby sources to reduce contamination of the region around 9io9 by strong sidelobes. The resulting image of 9io9, centred at 5\u2009GHz, has a 500\u00a0\u00d7\u00a0400\u2009mas beam (position angle, PA = 7\u00b0), with an rms noise level of 3\u20134\u2009\u03bcJy\u00a0beam<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=25864277 id=\"25864277\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-2>2.2 Sub-millimetre and millimetre</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">A large area of Stripe 82 has been mapped at 250, 350 and 500\u00a0\u03bcm with <em>Herschel</em>, as part of The <em>Herschel</em> Stripe 82 Survey (Viero et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>). At the position of 9io9, there is bright sub-millimetre detection, with <em>S</em><sub>250</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0826\u00a0\u00b1\u00a07, <em>S</em><sub>350</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0912\u00a0\u00b1\u00a07 and <em>S</em><sub>500</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0717\u00a0\u00b1\u00a08\u00a0mJy. The low resolution of <em>Herschel</em> cannot spatially resolve the emission, but the sub-millimetre colours are consistent with the emission originating from the background source at <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a02\u20133 (the lensing galaxy is not expected to be a significant source of sub-millimetre emission). In order to better constrain the shape of the far-infrared SED and determine the redshift of the lensed source, we obtained further sub-millimetre imaging and spectroscopy that we describe in the following.</p>                    <h4 scrollto-destination=25864279 id=\"25864279\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-2-1>2.2.1 James Clerk Maxwell Telescope SCUBA-2</h4>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We obtained a 30\u2009min observation of 9io9 with the SCUBA-2 camera on the James Clerk Maxwell Telescope (JCMT) on 2014 January 15 as part of Director's Discretionary Time (project M13BD12). SCUBA-2 records both 450 and 850\u00a0\u03bcm data simultaneously. We employed the DAISY scanning mode, appropriate for point sources, and the zenith opacity at 225\u2009GHz was \u03c4<sub>225\u2009GHz</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.07\u20130.1 over the course of the observation. The data were reduced with the dynamic iterative map maker of the <span class=\"small-caps\">smurf</span> package (Jenness et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>; Chapin et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) and optimally filtered for detection of point sources. We applied flux conversion factors (FCFs) of 491 and 537\u2009Jy\u00a0pW<sup>\u22121</sup> at 450 and 850\u00a0\u03bcm, respectively, which are the canonical FCFs derived from observations of hundreds of standard sub-millimetre calibrators observed by SCUBA-2 since operations began (Dempsey et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). An additional correction of 10\u2009per\u2009cent is made to the FCFs to account for flux lost during the filtering steps. The absolute flux calibration at 850\u00a0\u03bcm is accurate to within 15\u2009per\u2009cent.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">9io9 is detected with <em>S</em><sub>850</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0167\u00a0\u00b1\u00a04\u00a0mJy (the JCMT beam at 850\u00a0\u03bcm is 15 arcsec, and so this can be considered an integrated measurement). At the same position in the 450\u00a0\u03bcm map, we measure <em>S</em><sub>450</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0217\u00a0\u00b1\u00a072\u00a0mJy, this is a factor of 3 lower than the <em>Herschel</em> measured flux at approximately the same wavelength. Given the \u223c5\u00a0\u00d7\u00a0finer resolution of JCMT/SCUBA-2 compared to <em>Herschel</em>/SPIRE at 500\u00a0\u03bcm, it is possible that SCUBA-2 is resolving the emission into several beams, rendering some of the sub-millimetre emission undetectable. This offers a first insight into the distribution of cold dust in 9io9: higher resolution sub-millimetre mapping with a powerful interferometer such as the Atacama Large Millimeter Array will allow us to compare the dust and radio morphologies on comparable angular scales.</p>                    <h4 scrollto-destination=25864282 id=\"25864282\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-2-2>2.2.2 Large Millimeter Telescope AzTEC 1.1\u2009mm and Redshift Search Receiver</h4>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The Large Millimeter Telescope Alfonso Serrano, hereafter LMT, is a 50\u00a0m diameter millimetre-wavelength radio telescope on Volc\u00e1n Sierra Negra, Mexico, at an altitude of 4600\u00a0m. LMT \u2018Early-Science\u2019 observations were conducted towards 9io9 with AzTEC (Wilson et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>) and the Redshift Search Receiver (RSR; Erickson et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>), operating in the 1 and 3\u2009mm atmospheric windows, respectively. During the \u2018Early-Science\u2019 programme only the inner 32\u00a0m diameter of the LMT primary surface was aligned and coupled to the scientific instruments.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Continuum imaging of 9io9 was first conducted at 1.1\u2009mm with the AzTEC camera on 2014 January 16 <span class=\"small-caps\">ut</span>, and then repeated on 2014 January 24 <span class=\"small-caps\">ut</span> due to a poor focus in the original observations. The highest quality observations however were obtained during the early evening of 2014 November 2 <span class=\"small-caps\">ut</span> under improved weather conditions with a 225GHz zenith opacity of \u03c4<sub>225\u2009GHz</sub>\u00a0\u2248\u00a00.08. The measured beam-size at 1.1\u2009mm was 8.5\u00a0\u00d7\u00a07.9 arcsec<sup>2</sup> FWHM in azimuth and elevation, respectively. The AzTEC observations were made in the standard Photometry Map Mode using a Lissajous scanning pattern, and reduced using the standard AzTEC data-analysis pipeline (Scott et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>). Flux calibration was performed against Neptune, assuming a 1.1\u2009mm flux density of 18.38\u2009Jy. A short 4\u2009min AzTEC integration detected the source 9io9 with a signal-to-noise ratio, S/N=39 and a flux density of 95.5\u00a0\u00b1\u00a02.4\u00a0mJy, with no suggestion of extended emission. The accuracy of the absolute flux calibration is estimated to be \u223c10\u2009per\u2009cent.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The LMT operates with an ultra-wideband spectrometer, the RSR that covers the full 3\u2009mm atmospheric window (73\u2013111\u2009GHz) at low spectral resolution (31\u2009MHz channels, \u223c100\u2009km\u2009s<sup>\u22121</sup> at 90\u2009GHz). The RSR is a dual-beam dual-polarization spectrometer, with 38\u2009GHz instantaneous bandwidth, designed to detect molecular gas, typically CO line transitions, from high-redshift galaxies. A 1\u00a0h integration with the RSR was conducted towards 9io9 on 2014 January 19 <span class=\"small-caps\">ut</span> with a measured <em>T</em><sub>sys</sub> at 90\u2009GHz of 85\u00a0K. The RSR data were reduced and calibrated using the LMT <span class=\"small-caps\">dreampy</span> (Data REduction and Analysis Methods in PYthon) package. A single strong spectral-line was detected at 97.31\u2009GHz (Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a>). The combination of the photometric redshift of <em>z</em>\u00a0\u223c\u00a02.6, determined from the (sub-)millimetre and FIR observations presented in this paper, with the RSR spectrum eliminates alternative redshift solutions and any ambiguity in the measured spectroscopic redshift. We conclude that the detected line at 97.31\u2009GHz is <sup>12</sup>CO(3\u20132) from the lensed background source at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.553. Full details of the LMT observations towards 9io9, together with a more complete analysis of the AzTEC and RSR data, are presented in Sanchez-Arg\u00fcelles et\u00a0al. (in preparation) and Harrington et\u00a0al. (in preparation).</p>                        <a id=\"25864286\" scrollto-destination=\"25864286\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/452/1/10.1093/mnras/stv1243/2/m_stv1243fig3.png?Expires=1611467387&amp;Signature=mrxRjmoKgY6cagnW7~CoDXdqd0lqQRMO5vwWktp6SBl490GYoflEcEGc7ZjM1mA6-qGf~0zp-EleWnot8YoMhVaIjR5cPfj7lQwFMnIHDTC0fxu4PjAIXnBuhipsAcNLaweHQzx3~3ESYoFlxone1iGOkG6uYhQem4aLz2RGYuj5hbmHZHUy7GmrXQQAKtnpJmBQiq1vtSQBMluHNbrZS10PjNJ5lXCt5q5bDFD3LaAWu9sITg7dI8pE9asVRRjn51LPdxi3tId4xV94mJnXCAct7-mTYv~WatvZB-8LAXKq2XfBWTxYL~rrcQmBhXdz5NiP6U-BR3n7c6QJpiPIXA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The Redshift Search Receiver spectrum of 9io9 showing the full 38\u2009GHz instantaneous bandwidth spectral coverage in the 3\u2009mm atmospheric window (73\u2013111\u2009GHz). The detected molecular line at 97.312\u2009GHz is <sup>12</sup>CO(3\u20132) from the lensed background source at a redshift <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.553.</p></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=25864287 id=\"25864287\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2-3>2.3 Near-infrared spectroscopy</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We observed 9io9 with the Infrared Camera and Spectrograph (IRCS; Kobayashi et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2000</a>) instrument on the Subaru telescope on the night of 2014 January 15 <span class=\"small-caps\">ut</span> as a back-up target for the scheduled observing program.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn3\" data-open=\"fn3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>3</sup></a> The instrument was setup with the HK low-resolution grism covering the wavelength range 1.4\u20132.45\u00a0\u03bcm. We used the 0.9 arcsec wide slit of the \u2018Reflective 3W\u2019 slitmask, resulting in a spectral resolving power of <em>R</em>\u00a0\u2248\u00a095 at the central wavelength. The entrance slit was set at a PA of 155\u00b0east of north to align it to the bright, western half of the red ring. We observed 9io9 using the nod-on-slit technique with slit positions separated by 7 arcsec. We obtained 12 exposures for a total of 1\u00a0h on source. Calibration data comprised of argon lamp exposures for wavelength calibration, and halogen lamp exposures for flat-field calibration.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The data were reduced with the <span class=\"small-caps\">iraf</span><span class=\"small-caps\">twodspec</span> package, and standard procedures for flat-fielding and sky subtraction for the nod-on-slit technique were used. The final data product is a two-dimensional spectrum of 9io9 with a regular sampling along the spatial and spectral directions. To increase the S/N, we binned the data in spectral direction by a factor of 6\u00d7 and in the spatial direction by a factor of 4\u00d7. The total integrated spectrum of the western arc of the red ring of 9io9 is shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>. The redshifted nebular emission lines of H\u03b1+[N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] and (unresolved) [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] \u03bb\u03bb4959, 5007 and [S\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] \u03bb\u03bb6717, 6731 doublets are detected confirming the CO redshift of 2.553.</p>                        <a id=\"25864290\" scrollto-destination=\"25864290\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig4\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/452/1/10.1093/mnras/stv1243/2/m_stv1243fig4.png?Expires=1611467387&amp;Signature=Myl573oHO4oOgrSu36Cjcxpu7s4j3h6PINgCHr9RRJn45cNiGzEVYjPIKhTP9Ds8sVyF2XXIIBmcFEK7sO6HfSeHjXBZMasjq7cod7vkyADIV9Z-8ZCoKXQsAlXNSCQ-LRY-Y9MhfyE8OhAE99m0fVBh~wNdfZ82whfD9QkITumLUDrW9HKHEgTUd6lvvKVgS2SQdZvOkDMEQacFhcemXvIv~pjw3AMs87EY-f0Kc7kMqmd74~M3NrRz3~OjQhzlW0exTwqqfx~cdttE83R-lQdlKbr8Jj-V95ORktCiKRcwnYLB3LAD4upbM88Tz-NzmIOAHAfLZCGKn5FXGvAWIw__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Observed near-infrared HK spectrum of the primary arc of 9io9 taken with IRCS on Subaru. The low-resolution spectrum shows the clear rest-frame optical emission lines of the unresolved [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] \u03bb\u03bb4959, 5007 doublet, H\u03b1+[N\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>], and the [S\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] \u03bb\u03bb6717, 6731 doublet, confirming the redshift of the lensed galaxy at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.553 (see Section 2.3).</p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=25864291 id=\"25864291\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>3 ANALYSIS</h2>\r\n                    <h3 scrollto-destination=25864292 id=\"25864292\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-1>3.1 Spectral energy distribution</h3>\r\n<div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">In Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a>, we plot the (integrated) observed radio\u2013sub-millimetre SED of 9io9. We model the sub-millimetre\u2013millimetre photometry with a single temperature (<em>T</em>) modified blackbody <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ1\" content-id=\"equ1\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\nS_{\\nu }\\propto \\frac{(1-{\\rm e}^{-\\tau (\\nu )})\\nu ^3}{{\\rm e}^{h\\nu /kT}-1},\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(1)</span></div>where the optical depth \u03c4(\u03bd)\u00a0=\u00a0(\u03bd/\u03bd<sub>0</sub>)<sup>\u03b2</sup> is normalized to 100\u00a0\u03bcm (rest) and \u03b2 is the emissivity parameter. Since the redshift is accurately known, we allow \u03b2 and <em>T</em> to be free parameters. The best-fitting parameters are \u03b2\u00a0=\u00a02.2\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.4, <em>T</em>\u00a0=\u00a0(38\u00a0\u00b1\u00a04)\u00a0K which provides a good model of the observed emission over 250\u20131100\u00a0\u03bcm. The integrated (rest-frame) 8\u20131000\u00a0\u03bcm luminosity is \u03bc<em>L</em><sub>IR</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0(1.3\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1)\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>14</sup>\u2009L<sub>\u2299</sub>, where \u03bc is the lensing magnification. Note that this might underestimate the total infrared luminosity since we have not modelled mid-infrared emission from warmer dust components. We fit the radio portion of the SED with a power law <em>S</em><sub>\u03bd</sub>\u2009\u221d\u2009\u03bd<sup>\u2212\u03b1</sup>, with \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a01.11\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.05. The combined model SED is shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a>.</div>                        <a id=\"25864294\" scrollto-destination=\"25864294\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig5\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 5.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/452/1/10.1093/mnras/stv1243/2/m_stv1243fig5.png?Expires=1611467387&amp;Signature=JIMNa-EAcx-tCUJr44tPG5QR43HhgQjusfp3bQP0SEKq4idfBk1t64EO~v97DpNqskAliSfqF7urtXprr-aZjhHcb1XJSSJC3mK48-~IrJ6xdo9kNySnAROUAVLbL~gSZExPx5CaiGIamrCO7gW0Mnw~-HnxyrHjFtBtllVdqqfs8RIE6E8wT7fwm0ByQJo5-yx~L-MOdcbSN-Sj8ItgXBDRl1Wrp2~wtfyqIsUjzAGwIiVyyIbaILPOvWZmWEyksHWVEdNOgGJxln8mcWjhr5hXwN~9ysbbTxvyC4dgt5QwPTXnqE6pWVEyN39uNC-A9i8D0geOBS3v3im-bAZ3xA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Observed radio-to-sub-millimetre spectral energy distribution (SED) of 9io9, showing the integrated flux density of the lensed galaxy (we assume no contribution from the lensing galaxy at these frequencies). The SED is well modelled by a combination of modified blackbody with <em>T</em>\u00a0\u2248\u00a040\u00a0K, describing the cold dust thermal emission, and a simple power law \u03bd<sup>\u2212\u03b1</sup> at \u03bd\u00a0&lt;\u00a050\u2009GHz, with spectral index \u03b1\u00a0\u2248\u00a01.1, describing radio synchrotron emission.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig5\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 5.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/452/1/10.1093/mnras/stv1243/2/m_stv1243fig5.png?Expires=1611467387&amp;Signature=JIMNa-EAcx-tCUJr44tPG5QR43HhgQjusfp3bQP0SEKq4idfBk1t64EO~v97DpNqskAliSfqF7urtXprr-aZjhHcb1XJSSJC3mK48-~IrJ6xdo9kNySnAROUAVLbL~gSZExPx5CaiGIamrCO7gW0Mnw~-HnxyrHjFtBtllVdqqfs8RIE6E8wT7fwm0ByQJo5-yx~L-MOdcbSN-Sj8ItgXBDRl1Wrp2~wtfyqIsUjzAGwIiVyyIbaILPOvWZmWEyksHWVEdNOgGJxln8mcWjhr5hXwN~9ysbbTxvyC4dgt5QwPTXnqE6pWVEyN39uNC-A9i8D0geOBS3v3im-bAZ3xA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Observed radio-to-sub-millimetre spectral energy distribution (SED) of 9io9, showing the integrated flux density of the lensed galaxy (we assume no contribution from the lensing galaxy at these frequencies). The SED is well modelled by a combination of modified blackbody with <em>T</em>\u00a0\u2248\u00a040\u00a0K, describing the cold dust thermal emission, and a simple power law \u03bd<sup>\u2212\u03b1</sup> at \u03bd\u00a0&lt;\u00a050\u2009GHz, with spectral index \u03b1\u00a0\u2248\u00a01.1, describing radio synchrotron emission.</p></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=25864295 id=\"25864295\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-2>3.2 Lens modelling</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The system shows a primary lensing galaxy G1 and a secondary satellite galaxy G2, probably at the same redshift (<em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.206, see Figs\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> and <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a>). We choose an isothermal ellipsoid for the density profile of G1. Since G2 is located close to, and likely part of, the lens system, we model its density profile with an additional isothermal sphere. The parameters of the model are: positions and critical radius (<em>b</em>) for G1 and G2, ellipticity (\u03f5) and PA for G1 (\u03b8<sub>\u03f5</sub>), together with an external shear magnitude (\u03b3) and PA (\u03b8<sub>\u03b3</sub>). We use a Gaussian prior (\u03f5\u00a0=\u00a00.1\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1) on the ellipticity of G1 and a uniform prior on the position of G2 obtained from the optical light profile fitting of the galaxies.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The lens mass model is optimized to simultaneously fit the extended images from the <em>K</em><sub>s</sub>-band image (seeing \u03b8\u00a0=\u00a0(0.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1) arcsec) and the VLA 5\u2009GHz data where the lensed arc and the counter image are detected with sufficient signal to noise. The absolute astrometric calibration of the radio images is better than 100\u2009mas, and we cannot improve the relative alignment of the near-infrared and VLA images. Furthermore, we acknowledge that setting the source model to be coincident in the near-infrared and radio is an assumption that does not take into account the possibility that these have different source plane morphologies. However, it is necessary to make this assumption with the current data in order to reduce free parameters and degeneracies in the modelling. An annulus centred on the lens enclosing the arc and the counterimage is used as a constraint when optimizing the lens model. We model the background source in the near-infrared with a S\u00e9rsic profile (flux, position, ellipticity and PA, effective radius and S\u00e9rsic index) and in the radio with a Gaussian profile. We set the sources at both wavelengths to be coincident as we expect them to be physically associated.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">To construct the mass model, we use <em>gravlens</em> (Keeton <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>) and a Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) simulation for the fitting procedure (using the <span class=\"small-caps\">python</span> module <span class=\"small-caps\">emcee</span>; Foreman-Mackey et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) to optimize the parameters and explore degeneracies in the model. Initially, the position of G1 is held fixed during optimization and after a reasonable model is found the position is allowed to be free. The source plane model, model lensed images and residuals (for both wavelengths) are shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">6</a>. The magnification in the near-infrared is \u03bc<sub>NIR</sub>\u00a0\u2248\u00a011 and in the radio is \u03bc<sub>5\u2009\u2009GHz</sub>\u00a0\u2248\u00a013, the unlensed magnitude of the source is <em>K</em><sub>s</sub>\u00a0=\u00a020.75\u00a0mag and <em>S</em><sub>5\u2009\u2009GHz</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01.2 \u03bcJy\u00a0beam<sup>\u22121</sup>. The parameters of the best-fitting lens model are summarized in Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>. The critical radii from Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> can be taken with the lens and source redshifts to calculate the lens velocity dispersion for G1 and G2: \u03c3<sub>G1</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0316\u00a0\u00b1\u00a01 and \u03c3<sub>G2</sub>\u00a0=\u00a080\u00a0\u00b1\u00a04\u00a0km s<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p>                        <a id=\"25864299\" scrollto-destination=\"25864299\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig6\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 6.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/452/1/10.1093/mnras/stv1243/2/m_stv1243fig6.png?Expires=1611467387&amp;Signature=KffkqinYpEcTLn3wxFUVXPrB05CZGpMZbJU8Ipd0NIssGAJyfJIarrrrPkEbdbVh5ogg4~lDEUiufaSgaJPfrhIitkeB4xpcjSzRHhnmJLQrnYbf99Qge8Ag12FECmk3O6GnpdiXJ6wpgc5LV6jNvmwewZ4i4kJH6bIz-MnVQsBfgPg4o3XFNW5KuXhZEDr12jznijp464gMASXmpeyAfMirYY-PlwLP8CMlwhnRcZhvNPGgIP9aSJ1tu~ww~6dReZxHQS7KlEDVSa6sud1i~owDdhWW5Fe1iZ40LkuVOE0vIhGgi7FaitPm-d8qFHg5lk9TyoO7DciXnK0NDTbP-g__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Mass modelling results based on joint fitting of the near-infrared imaging (colour map) and 5\u2009GHz imaging (shown as contours at levels of 0.0033\u00d7(3,9,27)\u00a0mJy\u00a0beam\u22121). class=\"table-modal\"><table><thead><tr><th>Parameter<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: none;\">\r\n            .\u00a0</span></th><th>G1<span aria-hidden=\"true\" style=\"display: class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$-94_{-13}^{+9}$|</span>\u00a0</td><td><span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$377_{-32}^{+17}$|</span>\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td><em>y</em> (mas)\u00a0</td><td><span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$126_{-10}^{+4}$|</span>\u00a0</td><td><span class=\"inline-formula (\u03f5)\u00a0</td><td>0.12\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.02\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>PA (\u03b8<sub>\u03f5</sub>, deg)\u00a0</td><td><span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$84_{-1}^{+3}$|</span>\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>Shear magnitude (\u03b3)\u00a0</td><td><span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$0.064_{-0.004}^{+0.005}$|</span>\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td></tr><tr><td>Shear PA (\u03b8<sub>\u03b3</sub>, deg)\u00a0</td><td><span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$83_{-2}^{+1}$|</span>\u00a0</td><td>\u2013\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/25864300\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Although the eMERLIN data are of somewhat lower signal to noise, their higher resolution allows at least preliminary investigation of the radio source structure. Using the galaxy mass model established above for the <em>K</em><sub>s</sub>-band and VLA imaging, we can attempt to reconstruct the eMERLIN data using a parametrized Gaussian fit to represent the source. The results are shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig7\" data-open=\"fig7\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">7</a>. In general, the source reconstruction is subject to considerable degeneracy because the main constraint provided by the lensing concerns the source distribution around the high-magnification region corresponding to the caustic running across it. Nevertheless, the data indicate a double rather than a single Gaussian fit, if we believe the structure at the southern end of the arc which is difficult to reproduce with a single component. The best-fitting model is subject to some degeneracies with the existing data, which can be investigated using a standard MCMC procedure. Specifically, the relative position of the centres of the components has an error approximately equal to their separation in the best-fitting model, with the more compact component contributing most of the positional error. The parameters describing the compact component, including its precise position, are uncertain apart from its size, which needs to be below approximately 30 mas, or 250\u2009pc in the source plane. The more extended component is preferred to be elliptical (<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">\u2060|$b/a=0.27^{+0.15}_{-0.10}$|</span> with PA 15\u00b0\u00a0\u00b1\u00a024\u00b0), and together the radio structure is reasonably consistent with that of a small radio core and jet, although higher signal-to-noise data would be needed to make a more definitive statement, we defer this, together with a more systematic source reconstruction using other methods to a future work.</p>                        <a id=\"25864302\" scrollto-destination=\"25864302\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig7\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 7.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/452/1/10.1093/mnras/stv1243/2/m_stv1243fig7.png?Expires=1611467387&amp;Signature=ArsgyrlLxTvhqL~~ylNB9uZ5yGcp4Xgqx7j3CcER5QFJthACFRN9aKxpKWiTLQohTBn~cQUuIESq0KG0~EdL8SLbE2I27HRemfOfpctJlAAK1a-2IfxdVO4tdNCHet4o4M0-YqA1j3BOviZxFKV77YHiTA1Hw01HcjTSMFEr7dUrEfSpF7Qo~7VUOnfNQJGeWXK3OVnWLXI5VLk3QDE6VIov581PjNUJm27g4DrlgscNrgu29n5ZSEv7YqeynZSyOLBtvyDBjkqBHvxABzFjNX92RGK-qiS6KB4r6EF3EMe4n-4yGcvNo0LyVwG7WVbhkjXJZb2cuSYArDdSN-FQ0g__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Left: the reconstructed 1.4\u2009GHz source model, using a two-Gaussian fit and the lens model described in Section\u00a03.2. The source plane image is slightly offset from centre for illustration purposes. Images are orientated north up, east left.</p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=25864303 id=\"25864303\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>4 DISCUSSION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">By simultaneously fitting the <em>K</em><sub>s</sub>-band and 5\u2009GHz imaging we have derived a lens model that allows us to determine a magnification factor of approximately 10\u00d7. The magnification-corrected infrared luminosity of 9io9 is <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub>\u00a0\u2248\u00a010<sup>13</sup>\u2009L<sub>\u2299</sub>, ranking it amongst the most luminous galaxies in the Universe, in the \u2018hyperluminous\u2019 class. If the entire infrared luminosity budget is dominated by star formation (i.e. reprocessed ultraviolet emission from massive stars), then the implied star formation rate of 9io9 is <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\dot{M}_\\star \\approx 2500\\,\\mathrm{M}_{\\odot }$|</span>\u00a0yr<sup>\u22121</sup> (Kennicutt\u00a0&amp; Evans (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>), assuming a Chabrier initial mass function), a thousand times the star formation rate of the Milky Way. Given the radio flux density and reconstructed radio morphology described above, it is likely that 9io9 contains an active galactic nucleus (AGN) that contributes non-negligibly to the energy output, and therefore <span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">|$\\dot{M}_\\star$|</span> should be taken as an upper limit, with the actual star formation rate scaling like \u03b3<em>L</em><sub>IR</sub>, where \u03b3\u00a0\u2248\u00a00.6 for starburst/AGN composite systems at similar epoch with comparable <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub>, determined through mid-infrared diagnostics (Coppin et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>). Even with a 40\u2009per\u2009cent correction for AGN contribution to <em>L</em><sub>IR</sub>, the implied star formation rate is still remarkably high, at over a thousand solar masses per year.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">In the absence of other diagnostics (such as the observation of broad lines, or emission line ratios such as [O\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>]/H\u03b2), the infrared-to-radio luminosity ratio can be used as a basic diagnostic for the presence of an AGN, described by the parameter <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ2\" content-id=\"equ2\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\nq=\\log _{10}\\left(\\frac{L_{\\rm IR}/3.75\\times 10^{12}\\, {\\rm W\\,m^{-2}}}{L_{\\rm 1.4\\,GHz}/{\\rm W\\,m^{-2}\\,Hz^{-1}}}\\right)\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(2)</span></div>with the monochromatic radio luminosity <em>K</em>-corrected to the rest-frame 1.4\u2009GHz assuming the measured \u03bd<sup>\u22121.1</sup> spectral slope. Star-forming galaxies have a rather tight distribution of <em>q</em>, with \u3008<em>q</em>\u3009\u00a0\u2248\u00a02.5 and scatter of approximately \u03c3<sub><em>q</em></sub>\u00a0\u2248\u00a00.25 (e.g. Ivison et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>). The radio\u2013far-infrared correlation is thought to arise from two linked processes associated with massive star formation: (a) cosmic ray electrons from supernovae that emit synchrotron radiation as they are accelerated in magnetic fields, leaking out of dust-enshrouded H\u2009<span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> regions, and (b) infrared emission originating from dust-reprocessed ultraviolet photons emitted by young massive stars. In galaxies hosting AGN, synchrotron emission associated with black hole accretion can contribute to the radio flux, resulting in lower <em>q</em>. In this case, the magnification-corrected radio luminosity is <em>L</em><sub>1.4\u2009\u2009GHz</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0(1.7\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1)\u00a0\u00d7\u00a010<sup>25</sup>\u00a0W\u00a0Hz<sup>\u22121</sup>, implying <em>q</em>\u00a0=\u00a01.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1 (here we have assumed the same magnification correction for the radio and infrared emission, \u03bc\u00a0=\u00a013). Thus, 9io9 is not consistent with a pure starburst, with significant nuclear activity associated with the growth of the central black hole contributing to the radio emission.</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">The current data allow us to postulate, with a reasonable degree of confidence, the basic nature of the radio source. There are some hints that the radio spectral index is flatter in the core component, \u03b1\u00a0\u2248\u00a00.2 steepening to \u03b1\u00a0\u2248\u00a01 along the ring. This would be consistent with the classic picture of optically thick emission due to synchrotron self-absorption around a compact, dense nuclear source. The steepening spectrum could indicate aging of the relativistic electron population (which lose their energy as <em>E</em><sup>2</sup>) along a possible jet, but a more detailed analysis that takes into account the different visibilities sampled by eMERLIN and VLA, improved sampling of the radio spectrum and a more accurate lens model will be needed to investigate this. Considering the reconstructed source plane morphology, with compact core and potential jet/lobe structure, the radio power of 9io9 places it in the Fanaroff\u2013Riley II class of radio galaxies (<em>L</em><sub>1.4\u2009GHz</sub>\u00a0&gt;\u00a010<sup>25</sup>\u00a0W\u00a0Hz<sup>\u22121</sup>).</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=25864307 id=\"25864307\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5>5 SUMMARY</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Through a citizen science project \u2013 <span class=\"small-caps\">Space\u00a0Warps</span>\u00a0\u2013 we have discovered an extremely luminous galaxy at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a02.5, close to the peak in stellar mass and black hole growth in the Universe, lensed by a foreground LRG, possibly part of a small group (given the large Einstein radius compared to typical single-galaxy lenses) at <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.2. The lensed source, dubbed 9io9, appears as a red Einstein ring, prominent in the near-infrared, but very faint in the optical (and thus would have been overlooked in an optical survey), characteristic of either a heavily dust-obscured or predominantly old stellar population. This lensed system would have been overlooked in an optical survey. Archival and subsequent follow-up observations revealed that 9io9 is extraordinarily bright in the sub-millimetre, with a flux density approaching 1\u2009Jy at the peak of the thermal dust emission. We have resolved the lens at two radio frequencies, with sub-arcsecond eMERLIN and VLA mapping at 1.4 and 5\u2009GHz, respectively, revealing radio emission from the source tracing the near-infrared light. The strong radio and infrared emission indicates a galaxy undergoing an episode of rapid growth, either in an intense starburst episode, through significant matter accretion on to a central supermassive black hole, or both.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">A plausible evolutionary scenario for 9io9 is that the galaxy is destined to evolve into a massive (<em>M</em><sub>\u22c6</sub>\u00a0\u2248\u00a010<sup>11 \u2212 12</sup>\u2009M<sub>\u2299</sub>) elliptical galaxy by <em>z</em>\u00a0=\u00a00, perhaps located at the centre of a large group or cluster of galaxies. Seen when the Universe was less than 3\u2009Gyr old, we are gaining an insight into the major growth phase of this massive galaxy, with gravitational lensing providing a unique window on to spatial scales not possible for an equivalent non-lensed field source. The fact that high-luminosity sources such as this are intrinsically rare even in the absence of lensing highlights the benefits that citizen science can offer in the mining of (ever growing) astronomical imaging archives, albeit at the cost of rather complex selection functions for the objects found. We plan further follow-up of 9io9 to improve our understanding of the source. Particularly exciting is the possibility that we could detect and monitor variability in the source, which \u2013 if associated with the compact nuclear core \u2013 could be measured with cadences of days, weeks or months.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We thank the anonymous referee, whose comments improved the clarity of this work. This result would not have been possible without the help of thousands of volunteers participating in the project through BBC <em>Stargazing Live!</em> and the <em>Zooniverse</em>. We thank all of the Citizen Scientists who participated in <span class=\"small-caps\">Space\u00a0Warps</span>\u00a0. The full list of their <em>Zooniverse</em> usernames can be found at the following URL: <a class=\"link link-uri openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"http://spacewarps.org/#/projects/VICS82/summary\" target=\"_blank\">http://spacewarps.org/#/projects/VICS82/summary</a>. We thank Zbigniew \u2018Zbish\u2019 Chetnik, one of the volunteers who classified 9io9, who appeared on the <em>Stargazing Live!</em> programme to discuss the object. It is our pleasure to thank the BBC Stargazing Live production team for their efforts in making SpaceWarps a success: Liz Bonnin, Mark Thompson (presenters), Ross Fiddes, Gavin Hesketh, Amy Hewer, Ben Lucas, Abi Rose, Adam Toth (production team), Holly Salter, Lizzie Search, Lauren Webber (production co-ordinators), Clare Huggins (production manager), Laura Davey (production executive), Alex Dackevych, Simon Mackie (web team), Ayo Ajibewa, Faran Ismailpour, Ivan Lazic, Kiki Lawrence, Keaton Stone (researchers), Alastair Duncan (assistant producers), Tom Hewitson, Alice Jones, Penny Palmer, Sarah Sarkhel (VT directors), Chris Parkin, Paul Vanezis (producers), Ian Russell (director), Helen Thomas (executive producer) and Paul King (series producer).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">JEG is supported by a Royal Society University Research Fellowship. AM is supported by World Premier International Research Center Initiative (WPIInitiative), MEXT, Japan. Based on observations obtained with WIRCam \u2013 a joint project of Taiwan, Korea, Canada, France, and the Canada\u2013France\u2013Hawaii Telescope (CFHT) \u2013 and MegaPrime/MegaCam \u2013 a joint project of CFHT and CEA/DAPNIA \u2013 at CFHT and VIRCAM at the VISTA/ESO Telescope at the Paranal Observatory. The UMASS LMT group acknowledges support from NSF URO and ATI grants (AST-0096854 and AST-0704966) for the LMT project and the construction of the RSR.</p><div content-id=\"fn1\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn1\" class=\"end-note-link\">1</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\"><a class=\"link link-uri openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"http://zooniverse.org\" target=\"_blank\">http://zooniverse.org</a>.</p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn2\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn2\" class=\"end-note-link\">2</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\"><a class=\"link link-uri openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"http://talk.spacewarps.org/#/subjects/ASW0009io9\" target=\"_blank\">http://talk.spacewarps.org/#/subjects/ASW0009io9</a>.</p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn3\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn3\" class=\"end-note-link\">3</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">Proposal ID S13B-002.</p></div></span></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=25864317 id=\"25864317\" class=\"backreferences-title\" >REFERENCES</h2>\r\n<div class=\"ref-list\"><div content-id=\"bib1\" data-legacy-id=\"bib1\"><div class=\"refLink-parent\"><span class=\"refLink\"><a name=\"jumplink-bib1\" href=\"javascript:;\" aria-label=\"jumplink-bib1\"></a></span></div><div class=\"ref false\"><div id=\"ref-auto-bib1\" class=\"ref-content \"><div class=\"mixed-citation citation\"><p class=\"mixed-citation-compatibility\">\r\n<span class=\"person-group\">\r\n<div class=\"name\"><div class=\"surname\">Becker</div> <div class=\"given-names\">R. or footers": {"Overview": " class=\"chapter-para\">Although the eMERLIN data are of somewhat lower signal to noise, their higher resolution allows at least preliminary investigation of the radio source structure. Using the galaxy mass model established above for the <em>K</em><sub>s</sub>-band and VLA imaging, we can attempt to reconstruct the eMERLIN data using a parametrized Gaussian fit to represent the source. The results are shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig7\" data-open=\"fig7\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">7</a>. In general, the source reconstruction is subject to considerable degeneracy because the main constraint provided by the lensing concerns the source distribution around the high-magnification region corresponding to the caustic running across it. Nevertheless, the data indicate a double rather than a single Gaussian fit, if we believe the structure at the southern end of the arc which is difficult to reproduce with a single component. The best-fitting model is subject to some degeneracies with the existing data, which can be investigated using a standard MCMC procedure. Specifically, the relative position of the centres of the components has an error approximately equal to their separation in the best-fitting model, with the more compact component contributing most of the positional error. The parameters describing the compact component, including its precise position, are uncertain apart from its size, which needs to be below approximately 30 mas, or 250\u2009pc in the source plane. The more extended component is preferred to be elliptical (<span class=\"inline-formula no-formula-id\">\u2060|$b/a=0.27^{+0.15}_{-0.10}$|</span> with PA 15\u00b0\u00a0\u00b1\u00a024\u00b0), and together the radio structure is reasonably consistent with that of a small radio core and jet, although higher signal-to-noise data would be needed to make a more definitive statement, we defer this, together with a more systematic source reconstruction using other methods to a future work."}, "Action": "Scanned all subtables", "Status": "Complete"}}, "Paper text": "\r\n\r\n\r\n<!DOCTYPE
